Coercion (Ikrah) and the Validity of Talaq and Itaq
States Hanafi rule on validity of talaq/itaq under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing between revocable and irrevocable acts to determine the validity of talaq and itaq under coercion.

Core Idea
Irreversible acts like talaq and itaq are valid even under coercion in Hanafi law.

This passage from a Hanafi text explains a critical rule regarding coercion (ikrah). While some acts require consent and are invalid if coerced, others like divorce and emancipation are effective regardless. Understanding this distinction is essential for muftis handling cases of forced divorces or manumissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Recognize that talaq and itaq are irreversible acts (lazima) that cannot be annulled once pronounced.
Contrast with reversible acts like sale and marriage.
2
Distinguish between revocable and irrevocable acts
Hanafi law splits dispositions of a coerced person into those that can be revoked (ghayr lazima) and those that cannot (lazima).
Revocable: nikah, bay'; irrevocable: talaq, itaq.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
Coercion invalidates revocable acts but does not affect irrevocable acts; talaq and itaq are thus valid under coercion.
The reasoning: these are not contracts requiring consent but declarations severing rights.
4
Compare with opposing positions
The three Imams (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) hold that all coerced acts are invalid, citing the hadith of pardon for coercion.
Hanafi response: that hadith applies to sins, not to irreversible legal acts.
5
Determine the mufta bihi for fatwa
The Hanafi position (talaq/itaq valid under coercion) is the operative ruling for fatwa in the school.
This is the relied-upon view in later Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی کی زبان سے طلاق و عتاق کے الفاظ نکلوا لئے تو طلاق و عناق کا محقق ہو جائے گا جبکہ ائمہ ثلثہ مکرہ کے طلاق کے وقوع کے قائل نہیں ہیں۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ مکرہ کے وہ تصرفات جو نسخ کئے جاسکتے ہیں نافذ نہیں جیسے نکاح بیچ وغیرہ اور وہ تصرفات جو سیخ نہ ہو سکتے ہوں جیسے طلاق و عتاق وہ مکرہ کے بھی نافذ ہو جائیں گے بخلاف ع وشراء کے کہ ان میں رضامندی ضروری ہے اگر بجبر واکراہ بیع و شراء کی گئی تو وہ بیع وشراء صحیح اور درست نہ ہوگا لعدم الرضاء
If the words of divorce and emancipation are extracted from someone's tongue, divorce and emancipation will occur, even though the three Imams do not hold that divorce of a coerced person occurs. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the rule is that those dispositions of a coerced person which can be revoked are not effective, such as marriage, sale, etc., and those dispositions which cannot be revoked, such as divorce and emancipation, will be effective even from a coerced person, contrary to sale and purchase, because consent is necessary in them; if sale and purchase are done by force and coercion, that sale and purchase will not be correct and valid due to lack of consent.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage states a key Hanafi rasm rule regarding coercion (ikrah). It opens by noting that the three Imams (Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, Imam Ahmad) do not hold that talaq occurs under coercion. However, Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the revocability of the act. Acts that can be annulled (like marriage and sale) require consent and are invalid if done under coercion. But acts that cannot be annulled (like talaq and itaq) take effect even under coercion. The reasoning is that talaq and itaq are not contracts that require mutual consent; they are simply declarations that sever rights. The rule is that for talaq and itaq, the presence of coercion does not negate the effect in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مکرہ (mukrah): A person compelled to do an act under threat of harm.
اکراہ (ikrah): Coercion that negates consent but not capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, which of the following acts remains valid if done under coercion?
A) Nikah
B) Bay' (sale)
C) Talaq
D) Ijarah (lease)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, coercion does not invalidate irrevocable acts like talaq and itaq.

Talaq by One Ignorant of Word Meaning: Qada vs Diyanah
Rasm al-mufti rule on qada vs diyanah for talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qada and diyanah rulings for talaq uttered by an ignorant person and state the Hanafi mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Qada follows the outward word; diyanah requires inward intent.

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between what a judge enforces (qada) and what a person is accountable for before Allah (diyanah). This lesson explores a case where a person utters talaq words without understanding their meaning. The key legal question: does the talaq occur in qada, in diyanah, both, or neither? The answer turns on the role of intention and the authority of apparent expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ignorance
The case is a person who is taught talaq words in Arabic but does not know their meaning, then utters them. Recognize that the speaker lacks understanding of the words.
Ignorance here is of the meaning, not of the act of pronouncing.
2
State the three positions
List the three views: (1) Ibn Nujaym: absolute occurrence. (2) Faqih Abadali: occurrence in qada, not in diyanah. (3) Some mashayikh: no occurrence at all.
Note which view is attributed to whom.
3
Analyze the reasoning for qada vs diyanah
For Faqih Abadali: qada is based on the apparent meaning of words (subut al-hukm bi al-zahir). Diyanah considers inner intention (al-qasd wa al-niyyah). Here, since the speaker has no intention, diyanah does not impose the ruling.
This is a classic Hanafi distinction.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
The commentary indicates that Faqih Abadali’s view (qada occurs, diyanah not) is closer to the relied-upon view. This means a judge will enforce talaq, but the person is not sinful if he remarries without a fresh divorce.
The mashayikh’s view (no occurrence in either) is a minority position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف الطلاق والے مسئلہ میں اگر کسی کو عربی زبان میں طلاق و عتاق ، تدبیر وغیر کی تلقین کی دو ان کے معنی نہیں جانتا ہے اور اس نے ان الفاظ کو ادا کر دیا تو مصنف علیہ الرحمہ نے علی الاطلاق ان کے وقوع کا حکم لگایا ہے جبکہ فقیہ ابدالی فرماتے ہیں کہ قضاء تو طلاق وعتاق وغیرہ کا متفق ہو جائے گا لیکن دیا یہ نہیں اس لئے کہ جب وہ ان کے معنی مطلب ہی کو نہیں جانتا تو اس کا قصد و ارادہ ہی نہیں پایا گیا اس لئے دیا یہ وقوع طلاق دعتناق کا حکم نہیں ہو گا لیکن قضاء کا حکم ظاہر پر ہوتا ہے اور ظاہر میں یہ الفاظ وقوع طلاق و عتاق پر دال ہیں اور ان کے یہ معنی متعارف بھی ہیں لہذا ان کے ظاہری معنی پر محمول کیا جائے گا گو قصد وارادہ بھی نہ پایا جائے ۔ مشائخ اور جند فر ماتے ہیں کہ مذکورہ صورت میں قضاء ودیا یہ حکم کا ترتب نہیں ہوگا۔
Contrary to the issue of divorce: if someone is taught the words of divorce, emancipation, tadbir, etc. in Arabic while he does not know their meaning, and he utters those words, the author (may Allah have mercy on him) ruled absolutely that they occur. However, Faqih Abadali says: as for qada, divorce, emancipation, etc. will be agreed to occur, but diyanah not, because since he does not know their meaning at all, his intention and will are not found; therefore diyanah (religiously) the ruling of occurrence of divorce and emancipation will not be given. But qada's ruling is based on the apparent, and apparently these words indicate occurrence of divorce and emancipation, and these meanings are well-known, so they will be construed according to their apparent meaning even if intention and will are not found. The mashayikh and some jurists say that in the mentioned case, neither qada nor diyanah ruling will follow.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding a person who utters talaq words without understanding their meaning. The author (Ibn Nujaym) initially rules that talaq occurs absolutely. Then Faqih Abadali distinguishes between qada and diyanah: in a court (qada), the judge rules based on apparent words, so talaq occurs; but in conscience (diyanah), since the speaker had no intention, talaq does not occur. A third opinion from some mashayikh holds that neither qada nor diyanah applies, because the essential intention is missing. The passage illustrates the Hanafi principle that qada looks to outward expressions, while diyanah considers inner intent. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that qada enforces the apparent, but diyanah absolves the ignorant speaker.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): The judicial ruling based on apparent evidence.
دیانتاً (diyanatan): The religious or conscience-based ruling before Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what is the ruling for a person who utters talaq words without knowing their meaning?
A) Talaq occurs in both qada and diyanah.
B) Talaq occurs in qada but not in diyanah.
C) Talaq does not occur in either.
D) Talaq occurs in diyanah but not in qada.

Key Takeaway
For an ignorant speaker, qada enforces talaq based on apparent words, while diyanah does not due to lack of intention – a key Hanafi distinction.

Mispronounced Talaq Words (Musahhafah)
Rule on talaq uttered with distorted words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule that mispronounced talaq words are kinayah, and distinguish the rulings of the mufti (diyanah) and the qadi (qada).

Core Idea
Distorted talaq words are kinayah: intention matters for diyanah; qada rules on outward unless prior declaration.

In this lesson, we examine a classic rasm al-mufti rule: the treatment of mispronounced (musahhaf) words when uttering talaq. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the internal (diyanah) and external (qada) domains, applying the principle that outward acts are taken at face value unless a contrary intention is established beforehand. Understanding this distinction is critical for any mufti faced with a case of ambiguous talaq wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distorted words
First, recognize that the speaker used a mispronounced form of the word 'talaq', such as 'talagh', 'talaq', etc. These are not the standard explicit terms.
The standard explicit terms are 'talaq', 'sarahah' etc.
2
Classify as kinayah
According to the Hanafi school, such distorted words are classified as kinayah (implicit) because they deviate from the clear, established wording. Kinayah requires intention to effect talaq.
Explicit words (sarih) do not require intention.
3
Apply the kinayah rule
Since the words are kinayah, talaq does not occur in diyanah (before God) unless the speaker intended divorce. However, in qada (court), the outward manifestation is considered, so talaq is enforced unless the speaker provides prior evidence of his real intention.
The mufti gives fatwa based on diyanah; the qadi judges based on qada.
4
Distinguish mufti and qadi roles
The mufti looks to the inner intent and will rule no talaq in diyanah if no intention. The qadi looks to the outward and, without prior declaration, will rule talaq in qada. This is a key difference.
Prior declaration: the speaker must have told witnesses before the incident that he was only joking or threatening.
5
Identify the exception
If the speaker preemptively declared to witnesses that his distorted words were not meant as talaq, then even the qadi rules no talaq. The prior declaration shifts the outward evidence.
This exception balances the two domains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى قالوا ان الطلاق يقع بالألفاظ المصحفة قضاءً و لكن لا بد ان يقصدها باللفظ. إذا كانوا نے طلاق دی طلاق الفاظ کو بگاڑ کر طلاق دے مثلا یوں کہے تلاق ملاک تلاغ طلاک طلاغ تو اگر ان الفاظ کے استعمال کرنے والے نے طلاق کی نیت کی تو طلاق واقع ہو جائیں اور اگر وہ یوں کہے کہ ان الفاظ سے میرا مقصد طلاق دینا نہیں تھا تو دیانہ طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی لیکن قضاء طلاق واقع ہوجا ئیگی کیوں کہ یہ الفاظ کنائی کے حکم میں سے ہے لہذا نیت کی ضرورت پیش آئے گی یعنی مفتی تو طلاق کے عدم وقوع کا فتوی دے گا لیکن قاضی طلاق کے وقوع کا فیصلہ کرے گا البتہ اگر وہ پہلے ان الفاظ کے استعمال سے گواہ بنائے کہ ان الفاظ سے میرا مقصد طلاق دینا نہیں بلکہ اپنی بیوی کو ڈرانا دھمکاتا ہے تو پھر قضاء بھی عدم وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہوگا۔
Thus they said: divorce occurs through mispronounced words in qada, but one must intend them by the word. If someone gives divorce by distorting the words of divorce, e.g., saying 'talaq, malak, talagh, tilak, tilagh', then if the user intended divorce, divorce occurs. And if he says that his intention was not to give divorce by these words, then diyanah divorce does not occur, but qada divorce occurs because these words are in the category of kinayah, so intention is needed. That is, the mufti will give a fatwa of non-occurrence, but the judge will rule occurrence. However, if he first establishes witnesses that his intention by these words was not divorce but rather to scare or threaten his wife, then even qada will rule non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage deals with mispronounced (musahhafah) words for talaq. The jurists rule that such words effect divorce in qada if the speaker intended them as divorce. However, the ruling distinguishes between qada and diyanah. If the speaker had no intention of divorce, diyanah absolves, but qada still enforces the apparent meaning unless he preemptively declared his intention to witnesses. The rationale is that distorted words are akin to kinayah (implicit) because they are not the standard explicit terms. Therefore, intention becomes a factor. The mufti (giving a religious opinion) considers the inner intent, so he would say no divorce in diyanah. The judge (qadi) looks to the outward and general understanding, so unless the speaker has a clear contrary indication (like prior witness testimony), the judge rules divorce. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that outward acts are taken at face value unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
مصحف (musahhaf): A word that is distorted or mispronounced from its original form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the judge rule when someone uses mispronounced talaq words without prior declaration of intent?
A) Talaq does not occur
B) Talaq occurs only if the words are exactly correct
C) Talaq occurs in qada even without intention
D) Talaq occurs only in diyanah

Key Takeaway
Mispronounced talaq words are kinayah: intention governs diyanah, outward governs qada unless prior declaration indicates otherwise.

Talaq by a Forgetful Person: Qada vs. Diyana
Ruling on talaq by a forgetful person with qada/diyana distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing qada and diyana in talaq by a forgetful person, and evaluate the role of capacity for intent.

Core Idea
Explicit talaq from a forgetful person occurs in court but not before God unless intended.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair discusses a critical rasm al-mufti rule: the effect of forgetfulness on divorce. The author distinguishes between external legal ruling (qada) and internal spiritual reality (diyana), and explains why sleep differs from heedlessness. Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti when issuing fatwas in cases of forgetful divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling for talaq by a ghaafil
The passage states that if a heedless person utters explicit divorce words, the divorce occurs in qada (court ruling) regardless of intent, unless there is a qarina sarifa (diverting context). In diyana (before God), it does not occur unless he intended divorce.
This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi fatwa methodology.
2
Understand the reasoning: intent is internal
Intent is an internal matter known only to God. The judge rules on the apparent (zahir), and explicit words from a sane adult are taken at face value. Therefore, qada gives effect to the apparent.
3
Differentiate ghaflat from sahw and sleep
Ghaflat (heedlessness) means something leaves the mind but returns upon effort. Sahw (error) means memory does not return with effort. In sleep, the capacity for intent (ahliyyat al-qasd) is entirely absent because it depends on reason. Hence talaq during sleep does not occur even qadaan.
The author distinguishes these to clarify why qiyas cannot be made from sleep to ghaflat.
4
Analyze the qiyas argument
The passage anticipates an objection: if talaq in ghaflat is valid, why not in sleep? The response: the illa (effective cause) for validity is the presence of capacity for intent, which is absent in sleep. Therefore, the two cases are different.
This is an example of qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference).
5
Apply the rule in fatwa practice
When a mufti is asked about a divorce uttered in a state of forgetfulness, he must determine whether the person had capacity for intent at the time. If yes, he rules that the divorce is effective qadaan but not diyanan unless intended. This preserves both the external legal order and internal spiritual sincerity.
This is a key rasm al-mufti principle.

فلو طلق غافلاً : الفاظ صریح میں ان کے وقوع کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے بشرطیکہ کوئی قرین صارف ہوا دیانہ ان کے حکم کا وقوع نہ ہو گا اس پر تفریح پیش کرتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے غفلت یا بھول میں یا غلطی سے طلاق دیدی تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ رہی یہ بات کہ ان صورتوں میں طلاق واقع نہ ہونی چاہئے کیونکہ غافل یا نسیان میں بہتا لشخص یا خطی کا ارادہ طلاق کا نہیں تھا تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قصد وارادہ ایک امر باطنی ہے جس پر مطلع ہونا مشکل امر ہے۔ الہذا حکم ظاہر پر لگے گا اور ظاہر یہ ہے کہ ان الفاظ صریحہ کا استعمال کرنے والا عاقل بالغ ہے اور اس کے اندر قصد و ارادہ کی اہلیت ہے لہذا بغیر قرینہ صارفہ کے الفاظ صریحہ کو ان کے ظاہر سے نہیں پھیرا جائے گا۔

اگر کوئی اعتراض کرے کہ جس طرح غفلت میں الفاظ صریح سے طلاق دینے سے طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے تو مند میں بھی طلاق دینے سے طلاق واقع ہو جانی چاہئے کہ نیند میں بھی آدمی غفلت میں مبتلا رہتا ہے جو اب اس کا یہ ہے کہ حالت نوم میں اس کے اندر اہلیت طلاق یعنی قصد وارادہ مفقود ہے جس کا تعلق عقل سے ہوتا ہے بخلاف غفلت میں طلاق دینے کے کہ وہاں غافل میں اہمیت طلاق موجود ہے لہذا ایک کو دوسرے پر قیاس نہیں کر سکتے ۔
If he divorces while heedless: For explicit words, intention is not required for their occurrence, provided there is no diverting context (qarina sarifa). Diyana, the ruling will not occur. He elaborates: if someone gives divorce in heedlessness, forgetfulness, or mistake, the divorce will occur. As for the objection that in these cases divorce should not occur because the heedless or forgetful person did not intend divorce, the answer is that intent is an internal matter difficult to know. Therefore, the apparent ruling applies, and the apparent is that the user of explicit words is a sane adult capable of intent. Hence, without a diverting context, explicit words are not turned from their apparent meaning. Diyana, divorce will not occur until he intended his wife by the words of divorce. The author has placed this restriction here. Heedlessness means something leaves the mind and returns upon effort, while sahw (error) means that even with effort it does not come. The author's wording joined sahw to ghaflat, implying distinction, so this meaning is better, though some consider them synonymous. A definition is given: sahw is a mistake arising from lack of caution and vigilance. If one objects that just as explicit words of divorce in heedlessness cause divorce, so too in sleep divorce should occur because a sleeper is also heedless, the answer is that in sleep the capacity for divorce (intent) is absent, being tied to reason, unlike heedlessness where capacity exists. Hence one cannot analogize one to the other.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a rule of fatwa (rasm al-mufti) regarding explicit divorce words uttered by a forgetful person. The author clarifies that in qada (court ruling), the divorce occurs because the apparent meaning of explicit words is taken, and intent is not required. However, in diyana (conscience before God), the divorce does not occur unless the husband actually intended divorce. This is because the ruling on the apparent is for the judge, while internal reality is known to God. The passage then distinguishes ghaflat (heedlessness) from sahw (error) and explains why sleep is different: a sleeping person lacks the capacity for intent altogether, so divorce does not occur even qadaan. This is a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the capacity for intent (ahliyyat al-qasd) is a condition for the validity of divorce.

Key Terms
غفلت (ghaflat): Heedlessness: something leaves the mind but returns upon effort.
سہو (sahw): Error: a mistake due to lack of caution; memory does not return with effort.
قرینہ صارفہ (qarina sarifa): A verbal or situational context that diverts the explicit word from its apparent meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the difference between talaq in heedlessness (ghaflat) and talaq in sleep?
A) In heedlessness, talaq occurs qadaan but not diyanan; in sleep, neither occurs.
B) In both cases, talaq occurs qadaan but not diyanan.
C) In heedlessness, talaq occurs both qadaan and diyanan; in sleep, only qadaan.
D) In both cases, talaq does not occur at all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, talaq by a forgetful person is valid in qada due to capacity for intent, but not in diyana without actual intent.

Talaq with Distorted Words (Alfaz Mushaffaha)
Ruling on divorce using corrupted versions of talaq words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on distorted talaq words (alfaz mushaffaha) distinguishing between the mufti's fatwa (diyana) and the qadi's judgement (qada).

Core Idea
Distorted talaq words are kinayah: intention is key; mufti accepts the husband's inner state, qadi judges by outward signs.

This passage addresses a subtle mufti issue: when a husband uses a distorted version of 'talaq', like 'talak' or 'talagh', is the divorce effective? The Hanafi school classifies such words as kinayah (allusive), not sarih (explicit). Consequently, the ruling depends on intention. However, the mufti must navigate the tension between the husband's inner claim and the apparent usage. This lesson clarifies the mufti's position and the qadi's different role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the words as mushaffaha
The distorted words are not the standard 'talaq' but variations like 'talak', 'talagh', etc. Recognize that these are considered corrupted forms.
The standard term for divorce is 'talaq'. Any deviation like 'talak' or 'talagh' is mushaffaha.
2
Classify them as kinayah
Since they deviate from the explicit sarih word, they fall under kinayah. In Hanafi law, kinayah requires niyyah (intention) in both qada and diyana.
Kinayah words are allusive; the intention of the speaker determines whether the legal effect occurs.
3
Apply the general rule for kinayah
For kinayah, the person's intent is what counts in diyana (before Allah). If the husband claims he did not intend talaq, the mufti accepts that claim and rules no divorce.
Diyana (diyānah) means in conscience or religiously; it relates to the inner state of the individual.
4
Distinguish between mufti and qadi
The mufti's fatwa follows diyana: he accepts the husband's claim of no intent. However, the qadi judges by apparent circumstances (qada) and rules occurrence unless the husband pre-emptively clarified his intention.
Qada (qaḍāʾ) is the judicial ruling based on evidence and outward meanings.
5
Note the exception for preemptive declaration
If the husband declares before witnesses that he intended only to threaten (not divorce), then even the qadi rules non-occurrence, because the outward circumstance now supports his claim.
Preemptive clarification establishes a counter-evidence that the qadi can rely on.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى قالوا ان الطلاق يقع بالألفاظ المصحفة : اگر کوئی الفاظ طلاق کو بگاڑ کر طلاق دے مثلا یوں کہے تلاق ملاک تلاغ طلاک طلاغ تو اگر ان الفاظ کے استعمال کرنے والے نے طلاق کی نیت کی تو طلاق واقع ہو جائیں اور اگر وہ یوں کہے کہ ان الفاظ سے میرا مقصد طلاق دینا نہیں تھا تو دیانہ طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی لیکن قضاء طلاق واقع ہوجا ئیگی کیوں کہ یہ الفاظ کنائی کے حکم میں سے ہے لہذا نیت کی ضرورت پیش آئے گی یعنی مفتی تو طلاق کے عدم وقوع کا فتوی دے گا لیکن قاضی طلاق کے وقوع کا فیصلہ کرے گا البتہ اگر وہ پہلے ان الفاظ کے استعمال سے گواہ بنائے کہ ان الفاظ سے میرا مقصد طلاق دینا نہیں بلکہ اپنی بیوی کو ڈرانا دھمکاتا ہے تو پھر قضاء بھی عدم وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہوگا۔
Even they said that divorce occurs with distorted words: If someone corrupts the words of divorce and says, for example, 'talaq', 'malaq', 'talagh', 'talak', 'talagh', then if the user intended divorce, divorce occurs. If he says he did not intend divorce, then diyana divorce does not occur, but qadaan divorce will occur because these words are in the ruling of kinayah. Therefore intention is needed; i.e., the mufti will give a fatwa of non-occurrence, but the qadi will rule occurrence. However, if he first established witnesses that by these words he did not intend divorce but rather to frighten or threaten his wife, then qadaan also the ruling of non-occurrence will be given.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage discusses divorce using distorted versions of the word 'talaq' (like 'talak', 'talagh', etc.). These are considered kinayah (allusive) because they are not the standard explicit terms. For kinayah words, intention is required in both qada and diyana. However, the text distinguishes: if the husband claims no intent, the mufti (diyana) accepts his claim and rules non-occurrence, but the qadi (qada) rules occurrence because the claim contradicts the apparent usage. If the husband pre-emptively declares his intention (e.g., by telling witnesses he intends only to threaten), then even the qadi rules non-occurrence. This illustrates the principle that in qada, the judge relies on apparent circumstances, but in diyana, the mufti accepts the person's inner state.

Key Terms
الفاظ مصحفہ (al-alfaz al-mushaffaha): Corrupted or distorted versions of the standard divorce words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It is invalid regardless of niyyah.
D) It is considered a joke and has no effect.

Key Takeaway
Distorted talaq words are kinayah; the mufti's fatwa follows the husband's claimed intention, while the qadi rules by apparent usage unless intention was pre-emptively clarified.

Intention in Explicit Talaq: Qada vs Diyanah
Clarifies when intention matters in explicit divorce phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding intention in explicit divorce and apply it to scenarios of teaching, mistake, and jest, distinguishing between judicial and religious outcomes.

Core Idea
Explicit talaq does not need intention for judicial effect but does for religious validity.

This passage refines the well-known rule that explicit divorce words do not require intention. You will learn that this rule applies only to judicial rulings; religiously, intention matters. Exceptions like jest are based on textual evidence, not analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Start by noting the rule 'al-sarih la yahtaju ila al-niyyah' (explicit words do not need intention).
This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Examine the exceptions
Consider two scenarios: teaching divorce rulings and reading a written divorce without intent. In these, divorce does not occur at all because intent is absent.
These do not contradict the rule because the husband never intended divorce.
3
Distinguish qada vs diyanah
Understand that the rule applies to qada (judicial ruling). For diyanah (religious reality), intention is required. The mistaken utterance scenario illustrates: divorce occurs judicially but not religiously.
The judge rules based on apparent words; between the husband and Allah, intent is considered.
4
Analyze the jest exception
Note the objection: if mistaken utterance triggers judicial divorce only, why does jest trigger both? The answer is a hadith making jest serious in marriage and divorce.
This is a text-based exception overriding the analogy with mistake.
5
Synthesize the full principle
Combine: sarih talaq does not need intention for qada, but does for diyanah, except when the husband deliberately intends divorce or when the case is jest (textual exception).
The mufta bihi position is that the joker's divorce occurs both ways due to the hadith.

ولا ينافيه قولهم إن الصریح : اس پر یہ اعتراض نہیں ہونا چاہئے کہ صریح میں نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے لہذا اوپر ذکر کردہ دونوں مسئلے فقہاء کی عبارت ان الصريح لا يحتاج الى النية کے مخالف ہیں۔ جواب: اس کا یہ ہے کہ جب شوہر کی مراد الفاظ سریحہ سے طلاق دینے کی ہو تب اسے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے اور اوپر ذکر کردہ عبارت کر ر مسائل الطلاق اور کتبت امرائی طالق میں اس نے طلاق کا ارادہ کیا ہی نہیں ہے۔ وقالو الو قال انت طالق ناويا الطلاق من وثاق . اگر کسی شخص نے اپنی بیوی سے انت طالق کہا اور ٹسی چیز کی صراحت نہیں کی اب اگر وہ یہ کہے کہ میری نیت قید سے رہائی یا عمل سے چھٹکارے کی تھی تو دیا یہ تو تصدیق کرلی جائے گی اور عدم وقوع طلاق کا حکم نہ ہو گا لیکن قضاء تصدیق نہ ہوگی اور قاضی طلاق کے وقوع کا فیصلہ دے گا کیونکہ اس کا دعوی بلا کسی قرینہ کے ظاہر کے خلاف ہے اور دیانت تصدیق اس لئے ہوگی کہ چونکہ لفظ اس کا متحمل ہے اور محتمل نیت سے ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔ ولونوی به الطلاق عن وثاق دين وتحته في الشامية اى تصح نيته فيما بينه وبين ربه تعالى لانه نوى ما يحتمله لفظه الى قوله لانه خلاف الظاهر بلا قرينة. تشریح: بعض کتابوں کی عبارت سے یہ معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے سبحان اللہ کہنے کا ارادہ کیا مگر خطا اس کی زبان سے انت طالق نکل گیا تو اس کی بیوی پر قضاء طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی رایانه این فیما بین دومین اٹھا طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ صریح میں قضاء نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی البتہ دیا نہ طلاق کے وقوع میں نیت کی ضرورت پیش آتی ہے۔ ولا يرد عليهم فولهم : اس عبارت سے ایک اعتراض کا جواب دیا جا رہا ہے کہ جیسے خطاء کی صورت میں قصد وارادہ نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے الفاظ صریحہ سے قضاء طلاق واقع ہوتی ہے دیانیہ نہیں ایسے ہی ازراہ مذاق اگر کوئی اپنی ہوئی کوانت طالق کہہ دے تو قصد و ارادہ نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے قضا و طلاق واقع ہونی چاہئے نہ کہ دیانہ جبکہ فقہا و از او نان الفاظ صریحہ سے طلاق دینے کی صورت میں قضا ہو یا یہ وقوع طلاق کا حکم لگا دیتے ہیں۔ مصنف جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ قیاس کا تقاضہ تو یہی ہے کہ جو حکم خطا کا ہے وہی حکم مذاق کا بھی ہونا چاہئے لیکن چونکہ مذاق کے بارے میں صراحت نص موجود ہے اس لئے وہاں قیاس نہیں چلے گا نص مندرجہ ذیل ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ثلاث جدهن جد وهزلهن جد النكاح والطلاق والرجعة. ( سنن ابي داؤ اس (۲۱۹۴)
He said: there will be no divorce. And if he wrote 'imra'ati taliq' or 'anti taliq' and that woman said to him 'read it in front of me'... Translation: The jurists said: if in her presence he repeatedly explained the issues of divorce and each time he said 'anti taliq', then divorce does not occur on the woman due to the lack of intent from the word, and this contradicts not the jurists' statement that the explicit does not need intention. And the jurists said: if he said 'anti taliq' intending freedom from a bond, then according to religious belief it will not occur but according to judicial ruling it will occur. Explanation: Example of issues of divorce: if someone repetitively discussed divorce issues in the presence of his wife and each time for the purpose of explaining the issue to his wife he said 'anti taliq', then if he did not intend divorce by 'anti taliq', divorce will not occur; and if he intended divorce, divorce will occur. While quoting from a book he also said the words 'imra'ati taliq': then if he intended to divorce his wife by that, divorce will occur; otherwise not. "Wa law katabat imra'ati taliq": if a woman wrote on a paper 'imra'ati taliq' and told her husband to read it aloud to her and he read it aloud, then if the husband intended divorce divorce will occur; if he did not intend divorce it will not occur. "Wa la yunafihi qawlahum inna al-sarih": There should be no objection that the explicit does not need intention, so the two cases mentioned above contradict the jurists' phrase 'al-sarih la yahtaju ila al-niyyah'. Answer: This is because when the husband's intention by the explicit words is to divorce, then he does not need intention; but in the above-mentioned cases of repetition of divorce issues and writing 'imra'ati taliq' he did not intend divorce at all. "Wa qalu law qala anti taliq naawiyan al-talaq min wathaq": If a person said to his wife 'anti taliq' and did not specify anything, then if he says his intention was freedom from a bond or release from an action, then religiously he will be believed and the ruling of non-occurrence of divorce will apply, but judicially he will not be believed and the judge will rule the occurrence of divorce because his claim is contrary to the apparent without any evidence. And religiously he is believed because the word is capable of bearing that meaning and the potential becomes proven by intention. "Wa law nawwa bihi al-talaq 'an wathaq dinan wa tahtahu fi al-shamiyya ay tasihhu niyyathu fi ma baynahu wa bayna rabbihi ta'ala li'annahu nawwa ma yahtamiluhu lafzuhu ila qawlihi li'annahu khilaf al-dahir bila qarinah. (Shami Zakariyya vol. 4 p. 4212) "Wa fi ibarat ba'd al-kutub anna talaqa al-mukhti' waqi' qada'an la diyanatan" So it became clear from this that the explicit does not need it judicially but does need it religiously. And the objection based on their statement that if he divorced her jokingly it occurs judicially and religiously does not refute them because the Shari' (peace be upon him) made his jest serious. Translation: And in the expression of some books it is stated that the mistaken person's divorce occurs judicially, not religiously, so from this it became clear that the explicit does not need it judicially but needs it religiously. And the objection based on the jurists' statement that if he divorces her jokingly it occurs judicially and religiously, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) made his jest in that matter serious. Explanation: From the expression of some books it is known that if a person intended to say 'subhanallah' but mistakenly uttered 'anti taliq', then judicially divorce occurs on his wife but religiously between him and his Lord divorce does not occur. From this it became clear that the explicit does not need intention judicially, but regarding religious occurrence of divorce, intention is required. "Wa la yarid alayhim qawlahum": From this phrase an objection is being answered: just as in the case of mistake, due to lack of intent, explicit words cause divorce judicially but not religiously, similarly if someone jokingly says 'anti taliq' to his wife, due to lack of intent it should occur judicially but not religiously, but the jurists rule occurrence both judicially and religiously when divorce occurs via explicit words. The author answers that analogy would require the same ruling as mistake, but because there is an explicit text about jest, analogy does not apply. The text is: From Abu Huraira: the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Three things, their seriousness is serious and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and return (to wife).' (Sunan Abi Dawud 2194)
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage deals with the crucial distinction between judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) rulings concerning explicit divorce (sarih talaq). The author opens by presenting a scenario: a husband repeatedly says 'anti taliq' while teaching his wife divorce rulings, without intending divorce. In this case, no divorce occurs because the words were devoid of intent. Similarly, if a husband reads a written divorce statement without intent, no divorce occurs. These examples are then reconciled with the well-known rule that explicit divorce words do not require intention. The reconciliation is that the rule applies when the husband intends the words for divorce; in these examples, intent was completely absent. The passage then presents another scenario: a husband says 'anti taliq' but claims he meant freedom from a bond (e.g., release from captivity). Religiously, between him and Allah, his intention is accepted and divorce does not occur, but judicially the court does not accept his claim because it contradicts the apparent meaning without any supporting evidence. This leads to the general principle: explicit divorce words do not need intention for a judicial ruling of divorce, but they do need intention for the divorce to be valid in the sight of Allah. The passage anticipates an objection: if this is the case, then why does a joker's divorce occur both judicially and religiously? Answer: because a specific hadith declares the joke to be serious in matters of marriage and divorce, overriding the analogy with mistake. Thus, the joker's divorce is an exception based on text (nass), not analogy.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Explicit, unambiguous words indicating divorce, such as 'anti taliq' or 'imra'ati taliq'.
قضاء (qada'): Judicial ruling by a judge based on apparent evidence; outward effect.
دیانة (diyanah): Religious reality between the person and Allah; internal validity based on truth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'anti taliq' while intending to teach his wife the rulings of divorce, and he does not intend divorce, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Divorce occurs both judicially and religiously.
B) Divorce does not occur because intent was absent.
C) Divorce occurs judicially but not religiously.
D) Divorce occurs religiously but not judicially.

Key Takeaway
Explicit talaq words trigger judicial divorce without intention, but religious validity requires intention; jest is an exception based on a hadith.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Kinayah in Talaq: The Mufti's Framework for Intention and Custom
Explains the general rule for kinayah and the custom exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for kinayah words in talaq, distinguishing between diyanah and qada', and identify when custom ('urf) makes a kinayah word operate as sarih.

Core Idea
Kinayah words require intention before Allah, but in court prior discussion suffices—except when custom makes a word explicit.

Kinayah (implicit) words for talaq are ambiguous; they can mean divorce or something else. The Hanafi school has a detailed framework determining when such words effect divorce, balancing the mufti's reliance on apparent circumstances (qada') and the husband's inner intent (diyanah). This lesson unpacks that framework, including the powerful role of custom that can transform a kinayah into a sarih (explicit) word.

Guided Path
1
Determine the type of word
First, classify the husband's utterance as sarih (explicit, e.g., 'talaq') or kinayah (ambiguous, e.g., 'udhhabi' or 'haram'). Only kinayah requires further analysis.
Sarih words always effect talaq regardless of intention.
2
Check for custom ('urf)
If the kinayah word is commonly used for talaq in the husband's community, it becomes sarih by custom and no intention is needed. This is based on the maxim 'al-thabit bil-urf ka al-thabit bil-nass' (what is established by custom is like what is established by textual evidence).
The passage gives the example of 'haram' when the community uses it for talaq.
3
Determine the forum: diyanah vs. qada'
In diyanah (before Allah), kinayah requires the husband's actual intention (niyyah) to effect talaq. In qada' (court), prior discussion of talaq (mudhakara) between the spouses suffices as a substitute for intention.
Mudhakara does not replace intention in diyanah.
4
Consider contextual qara'in (indications)
Even without mudhakara, a clear qarina (indication) such as an explicit statement of intent or a quarrel about talaq can establish talaq in qada'.
The passage uses the example of 'udhhabi' said during a dispute.

ہے اور عورت کو صفت طالق کے ساتھ متصف کرنے کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ بطور اقتضاء طلاق مقدر مانی جائے اور میخندی میں ہمارے نزدیک عموم نہیں ہوتا اور مقتضی کو ہم نے کلام کی صحت کے لئے مقدر مانا ہے اور کلام ایک طلاق بھی سے صحیح ہو گیا تو اب دو یا تین کی ضرورت باقی نہیں رہی۔ لہذا تین کی نیت ایسے معنی کی نیت کہلائے گی کہ لفظ جس کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا لہذا تین کی نیت درست نہ ہوگی ۔
ولا تصح نبة الثنتين في المصدر. اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی کوانت الطلاق کہا تو اس صورت میں ایک طلاق ربھی واقع ہوگی اور اگر تین کی نیت کر لی تو تین بھی واقع ہو جائیں گی کیونکہ الطلاق مصدر ہے اور مصدر میں تو حد ہوتا ہے اور توحد کی دو قسمیں ہیں ۔ (۱) ذاتی (۲) نوعی ۔ لہذا ذاتی بدون نیت کے متحقق ہو جائے گا البتہ مصدر چونکہ توحد عنی کو بھی محتمل ہے اور متل کے اثبات کے لئے نیت درکار ہے لہذا اس سے تین طلاقیں اس صورت میں واقع ہونگی جبکہ قائل نے تین کی نسبت کی ہو بدون نیت تین ثابت نہ ہونگی۔
البتہ دو کی نیت درست نہ ہو گی چونکہ دو عدد ہے اور مصدر تو صد پر دلالت کرتا ہے عد د اور تو حد میں منافات ہے اور کسی بھی لفظ سے اس کی ضد کو مراد نہیں لیا جا سکتا۔ البتہ اگر بیوی باندی ہو تو پھر انت الطلاق میں دو بھی طلاق مراد لی جاسکتی ہیں کیونکہ باندی کے حق میں دو وحدت نوعی ہیں۔
وَأَمَّا كناياتُهُ فَلا يَقَعُ بِهَا إِلَّا بِالنِّيَّةِ دِيَانَةً سَوَاءٌ كَانَ مَعَهَا مُذَاكَرَةُ الطَّلَاقِ أَوْ لَا وَالْمُذَاكَرَةُ إنَّمَا تَقُوْمُ مَقَامَ النِّيَّةِ فِى الْقَضَاءِ إِلَّا فِى لَفْظ الْحَرَامِ فَإِنَّهُ كِتَايَةٌ وَلَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهَا فَيُنْصَرَفْ إلى الطلاق إذَا كَانَ الزَّوْجُ مِنْ قَوْمٍ يُرِيدُونَ بِالْحَرَامِ الطلاق.
ترجمہ: اور بہر حال اس کے کنایات تو ان کے ذریعہ نیت سے دیا یہ واقع ہو گی خواہ ان کے ساتھ مذاکرہ طلاق ہو یا نہ ہو اور مذاکرونیت کے قائم مقام صرف قضا میں ہوتا ہے علاوہ لفظ حرام کے اس لئے کہ وہ کنائی ہے اور اس میں اس کی ضرورت نہیں پس اس کو طلاق کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا جبکہ شوہر ایسے قوم سے ہو جو حرام سے طلاق مراد لیتے ہیں۔
تشریح : الكناية لغةً شئ يستدل به علی غیره او یراد به غیره وشريعة ما استتر في نفسه معناه الحقيقى او المجازى فان الحقيقة المهجورة كناية كالمجاز الغالب الاستعمال. (شرح الحموي مع الاشباه والنظائر ص ٧٩) کنایہ اطلاق سے مراد یہ ہے کہ وہ طلاق اور غیر طلاق دونوں کا احتمال رکھتا ہو پس کنا یا اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی مراد پوشیدہ ہو بہ علامہ ابن نجیم المصری علیہ الرحمہ فرماتے ہیں کہ الفاظ کنائی سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے دیایہ نیت کا پایا جاتا ضروری ہے خواہ ندا کرۂ طلاق ہو یا نہ ہو ۔ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا اذہبی اور اس سے پہلے آپس میں دونوں میں تو تو میں میں ہور ہی تھی تو اگر چہ اسی صورت میں قضا ، تو وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہو گا خواہ شوہر طلاق کی نیت سے انکار کرے لیکن مائینہ طارق اسی وقت معتبر ہوگی جب شوہر یوں کہے کہ میں نے اذہبی سے طلاق ہی مراد لی ہے اور اگر اس نے طلاق کی نیت نہ کی تو دیانیت عدم وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہوگا۔
قضاء الفاظ کنائی سے طلاق اس وقت مراد ہوگی جب وہ نیت طلاق کی کرے یا کوئی قرینہ صراحت یا دالیا پایا جائے مثلا وہ صراحتہ کہے کہ اذ ہی سے میں نے طلاق ہی مراد لی ہے یا دونوں میں جھگڑا ہور ہا تھا اور آپس میں طارق کا ندا کرہ ہو رہا تھا اور ایسی حالت میں شوہر نے بیوی سے کہہ دیا اذ ہی تو قضاء وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہو گا بھلے شو ہر طارق کی نسیت کا انکار کرے کیونکہ قضاء تو ظاہر پر نافذ ہوتی ہے اور یہ دلالت حال ہی طلاق کے معنی کو ظاہر کر رہی ہے اور اگر شوہر نے اس سے طلاق کی نیت کی ہے تو دیا یہ بھی وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہو گا اس لئے کہ جب دریا ئیہ وقوع طلاق کا نظم ہے تو قضاء بدرجہ اول طلاق کا حکم ہوگا۔
دیا یہ وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے مذاکر کو طلاق ہو یا نہ ہو دونوں برابر ہیں۔ المذاكرة انما تقوم مقام النية الخ. اس عبارت سے صاحب کتاب یہ بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ الفاظ کنائی سے قضاء وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت ہو اور اگر نیت نہ ہو تو قرینہ یعنی مذاکرۂ طلاق وغیرہ ہو اس لئے کہ مذاکرۂ طلاق الفاظ کنائی سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے مثل نیت کے ہے۔ البتہ لفظ حرام اگر چہ الفاظ کنائی میں سے ہے لیکن یہ لفظ طلاق کے معنی میں صریح ہے اس لئے اس سے فورا طلاق کے سلسلہ میں نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ اور یہ دراصل استثناء ہے مصنف کی عبارت لا يقع بها الاسبانية سے لیکن اگر اس سے آپ طلاق بائن مراد لیں گے تو بینونت کے لئے نیت ضروری ہوگی اس لئے کہ مینونت کے اعتبار سے یہ الفاظ کنائی میں سے ہے اور الفاظ کنائی سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت ضروری ہے اور طلاق کے معنی میں مورتا ہے اس لئے لفظ حرام سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت پیش نہیں آئے گی ۔
اذا كان الزوج من قوم يريدون بالحرام الطلاق. اس عبارت سے ایک ضابطہ عرف کا بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ کوئی لفظ کنائی اگر کسی مقام پر یا کسی قبیلہ میں لفظ طلاق کے معنی میں صریح ہو تو وہاں عرف کا اعتبار ہوگا اور اس نقط کنائی سے اس مقام وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت پیش نہیں آئے گی اس لئے کہ اصول فقہ کا قائدا ؟
الثابت بالعرف كالثابت بالنص
وَأَمَّا تَفْوِيْضُ الطَّلَاقِ وَالْخَلْعُ وَالإيْلاءُ والظَّهَارُ فَمَا كَانَ مِنْهُ صَرِيْحاً لَا تُشْتَرَطَ لَهُ النَّبه
وَمَا كَانَ كِنَايَةً أُشْتَرِطَتْ لَهُ.
And it is necessary that the woman be described with the attribute of talaq by way of implication, and talaq is estimated to be intended. And in our madhhab, there is no generality, and we have estimated the implication for the correctness of speech. Since the speech is correct with one talaq, there remains no need for two or three. Therefore, intending three is intending a meaning that the word does not admit, so intending three is not valid. And the intention of two in the masdar is not valid. If someone says to his wife 'Anti al-talaq', then one talaq occurs, and if he intends three, three also occur because 'al-talaq' is a masdar and in the masdar there is unity, and unity is of two types: (1) essential (dhati) (2) generic (naw'i). So essential unity is realized without intention, but since the masdar also admits generic unity, for its affirmation intention is required. Thus three talaqs occur only when the speaker intends three; without intention three are not established. However, intention of two is not valid because two is a number and the masdar indicates unity, and number and unity are contradictory, and one cannot intend the opposite of a word. But if the wife is a slave, then in 'Anti al-talaq' two talaqs can be intended because for a slave two are generic unity. And as for his kinayah words, talaq does not occur by them except with intention in diyanah, whether or not there was prior discussion of talaq. And prior discussion (mudhakara) takes the place of intention in qada' except for the word 'haram', for it is kinayah and does not need it; it is turned to talaq when the husband is from a people who intend talaq by 'haram'. Translation: And however his kinayah, talaq occurs by them with intention in diyanah, whether or not there was mudhakara of talaq. And mudhakara stands in place of intention only in qada', except for the word 'haram' because it is kinayah and does not need it; so it is turned to talaq when the husband is from a people who intend talaq by 'haram'. Commentary: Kinayah linguistically is something by which something else is inferred or intended. Legally, it is what conceals its real or metaphorical meaning, for the abandoned literal meaning is kinayah like the dominant metaphorical usage. (Sharh al-Hamawi with al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, p. 79). By kinayah is meant that it admits both talaq and non-talaq. So kinayah is that whose meaning is hidden. Allamah Ibn Nujaym al-Misri (may Allah have mercy on him) says that for occurrence of talaq by kinayah words, intention is necessary in diyanah, whether there was mudhakara of talaq or not. If someone says to his wife 'Udhhabi' and before that there was a dispute between them, then in qada' the ruling of talaq will be given even if the husband denies intention. But in diyanah, his statement is accepted only if he says 'I intended talaq by udhhabi'; if he did not intend talaq, then in diyanah the ruling is non-occurrence of talaq. In qada', talaq is intended by kinayah words when he makes intention of talaq or there is a clear qarina (indication) — for example, he explicitly says 'I intended talaq by udhhabi' or there was a quarrel and discussion of talaq between them, and in such a state the husband said 'udhhabi' to his wife, then in qada' the ruling of talaq occurs even if the husband denies intention because qada' is based on apparent circumstances, and this contextual indication shows the meaning of talaq. And if the husband intended talaq, then in diyanah also the ruling of talaq occurs because when diyanah is the foundation of occurrence of talaq, then a fortiori qada' will rule talaq. In diyanah, for occurrence of talaq, intention is necessary, whether mudhakara of talaq existed or not, both are equal. 'Al-mudhakara innama taqumu maqam al-niyyah' etc. With this phrase, the author explains that for occurrence of talaq by kinayah in qada', intention is required, and if there is no intention, then qarina like mudhakara of talaq suffices because mudhakara of talaq stands in place of intention for kinayah. However, the word 'haram' although from kinayah, is clear in the meaning of talaq, so for talaq it does not require intention. This is actually an exception to the author's statement 'la yaqa'u biha illa bil-niyyah'. But if one intends talaq ba'in by it, then for ba'in intention is necessary because in terms of ba'in it is from kinayah, and for kinayah words intention is required for occurrence of talaq. Since it is commonly used in the meaning of talaq, for occurrence of talaq by the word 'haram' no intention is needed. 'Idha kana al-zawju min qawmin yuriduna bil-haram al-talaq': With this phrase, a rule of custom is being explained: if a kinayah word in a place or in a tribe is clear in the meaning of talaq, then custom will be considered, and that kinayah will not require intention for occurrence of talaq in that place, because the principle of usul al-fiqh: Al-thabit bil-urf ka al-thabit bil-nass (What is established by custom is like what is established by textual evidence). And as for tafwid al-talaq, khul', ila', and zihar: whatever of them is explicit, intention is not required for it, and whatever is kinayah, intention is required for it.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule discussing the conditions for talaq to occur with kinayah (implicit) words. The author begins by addressing a specific case: if a husband says 'Anti al-talaq' (You are divorce), which is a masdar (verbal noun). According to Hanafi fiqh, this expression results in one talaq automatically due to the essential unity of the masdar. If the husband intends three talaqs, they occur because the masdar can also indicate generic unity, but that requires intention. However, intending two talaqs is invalid because two is a number contradicting the unity implied by the masdar, unless the wife is a slave, where two is considered generic unity. The passage then turns to the general rule for kinayah: such words require niyyah (intention) for talaq to take effect in diyanah (in the sight of Allah), regardless of whether the couple had been discussing talaq (mudhakara). However, in qada' (court), mudhakara takes the place of niyyah. The exception is the word 'haram': if the husband's community customarily uses 'haram' to mean talaq, then no intention is needed; it is treated as sarih (explicit). The author then defines kinayah linguistically and legally, citing Ibn Nujaym, and elaborates with examples like saying 'Udhhabi' (Go away). If there is a prior dispute or context indicating talaq, the court rules talaq occurred, even if the husband denies intention. But in diyanah, his word is taken unless he concedes intention. Finally, the passage introduces the maxim 'Al-thabit bil-urf ka al-thabit bil-nass' (What is established by custom is like what is established by textual evidence), applying it to the word 'haram'. The passage concludes by listing other types of divorce (tafwid, khul', ila', zihar) with the same rule: explicit forms need no intention, kinayah forms need intention. Throughout, the focus is on the practical fatwa rules for a mufti or judge determining the occurrence of talaq based on apparent circumstances and custom.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): A word that has a clear and unambiguous meaning for divorce, requiring no intention.
كناية (kinayah): A word that is ambiguous, capable of meaning talaq or something else, thus requiring intention or qarina for talaq.
نية (niyyah): Mental intent of the husband to effect divorce when using kinayah words.
مذاكرة (mudhakara): Prior discussion or context of divorce between spouses that indicates intent.
عرف (urf): Customary usage of a community that can transform a kinayah word into an explicit one for talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary factor that determines whether the word 'haram' requires intention for talaq?
A) Whether the word is linguistically kinayah or sarih
B) The presence of mudhakara prior to the utterance
C) The customary usage of the husband's community
D) The husband's explicit statement of intention

Key Takeaway
For kinayah words, intention is key in diyanah, but court rulings rely on context and custom can make a word explicit.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Intending Numbers in Talaq: The Masdar Rule
Explains the rule for number of talaqs when using a masdar phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine how many talaqs occur when a husband uses a masdar expression like 'Anti al-talaq', based on his intention and the type of unity (essential vs. generic).

Core Idea
A masdar like 'al-talaq' gives one talaq automatically, but can give three by intention; two is impossible unless the wife is a slave.

The expression 'Anti al-talaq' (You are divorce) is a masdar (verbal noun) that inherently indicates a single occurrence of the action. However, the Hanafi school recognizes that a masdar can also signify generic unity, allowing the husband to intend three talaqs. But why can he not intend two? This lesson unpacks the linguistic logic and the exceptional case of a slave wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression as a masdar
Words like 'al-talaq' are masdars. Masdars inherently indicate unity of the action (one occurrence). This is called essential unity (al-wahdah al-dhatiyyah).
Essential unity is automatically present without intention.
2
Understand the two types of unity
Essential unity gives one talaq. But a masdar can also indicate generic unity (al-wahdah al-naw'iyyah), which refers to the whole genus or type. This is not automatic; it requires intention.
Generic unity can mean three talaqs because three is the maximum number of talaqs that can be given at once (except for slave).
3
Apply intention for three talaqs
If the husband intends three talaqs, generic unity is achieved, so all three occur. Without intention, only one occurs.
The intention of three is valid because it does not contradict the unity of the masdar; generic unity encompasses the whole set.
4
Rule out intention for two talaqs
Two is a specific number that contradicts the fundamental unity of the masdar. One cannot intend a meaning opposite to the word's primary indication. Therefore, intending two is invalid, and only one talaq occurs.
An exception exists if the wife is a slave: two talaqs constitute generic unity for a slave because a slave can only be divorced twice.

ہے اور عورت کو صفت طالق کے ساتھ متصف کرنے کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ بطور اقتضاء طلاق مقدر مانی جائے اور میخندی میں ہمارے نزدیک عموم نہیں ہوتا اور مقتضی کو ہم نے کلام کی صحت کے لئے مقدر مانا ہے اور کلام ایک طلاق بھی سے صحیح ہو گیا تو اب دو یا تین کی ضرورت باقی نہیں رہی۔ لہذا تین کی نیت ایسے معنی کی نیت کہلائے گی کہ لفظ جس کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا لہذا تین کی نیت درست نہ ہوگی ۔
ولا تصح نبة الثنتين في المصدر. اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی کوانت الطلاق کہا تو اس صورت میں ایک طلاق ربھی واقع ہوگی اور اگر تین کی نیت کر لی تو تین بھی واقع ہو جائیں گی کیونکہ الطلاق مصدر ہے اور مصدر میں تو حد ہوتا ہے اور توحد کی دو قسمیں ہیں ۔ (۱) ذاتی (۲) نوعی ۔ لہذا ذاتی بدون نیت کے متحقق ہو جائے گا البتہ مصدر چونکہ توحد عنی کو بھی محتمل ہے اور متل کے اثبات کے لئے نیت درکار ہے لہذا اس سے تین طلاقیں اس صورت میں واقع ہونگی جبکہ قائل نے تین کی نسبت کی ہو بدون نیت تین ثابت نہ ہونگی۔
البتہ دو کی نیت درست نہ ہو گی چونکہ دو عدد ہے اور مصدر تو صد پر دلالت کرتا ہے عد د اور تو حد میں منافات ہے اور کسی بھی لفظ سے اس کی ضد کو مراد نہیں لیا جا سکتا۔ البتہ اگر بیوی باندی ہو تو پھر انت الطلاق میں دو بھی طلاق مراد لی جاسکتی ہیں کیونکہ باندی کے حق میں دو وحدت نوعی ہیں۔
وَأَمَّا كناياتُهُ فَلا يَقَعُ بِهَا إِلَّا بِالنِّيَّةِ دِيَانَةً سَوَاءٌ كَانَ مَعَهَا مُذَاكَرَةُ الطَّلَاقِ أَوْ لَا وَالْمُذَاكَرَةُ إنَّمَا تَقُوْمُ مَقَامَ النِّيَّةِ فِى الْقَضَاءِ إِلَّا فِى لَفْظ الْحَرَامِ فَإِنَّهُ كِتَايَةٌ وَلَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهَا فَيُنْصَرَفْ إلى الطلاق إذَا كَانَ الزَّوْجُ مِنْ قَوْمٍ يُرِيدُونَ بِالْحَرَامِ الطلاق.
ترجمہ: اور بہر حال اس کے کنایات تو ان کے ذریعہ نیت سے دیا یہ واقع ہو گی خواہ ان کے ساتھ مذاکرہ طلاق ہو یا نہ ہو اور مذاکرونیت کے قائم مقام صرف قضا میں ہوتا ہے علاوہ لفظ حرام کے اس لئے کہ وہ کنائی ہے اور اس میں اس کی ضرورت نہیں پس اس کو طلاق کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا جبکہ شوہر ایسے قوم سے ہو جو حرام سے طلاق مراد لیتے ہیں۔
تشریح : الكناية لغةً شئ يستدل به علی غیره او یراد به غیره وشريعة ما استتر في نفسه معناه الحقيقى او المجازى فان الحقيقة المهجورة كناية كالمجاز الغالب الاستعمال. (شرح الحموي مع الاشباه والنظائر ص ٧٩) کنایہ اطلاق سے مراد یہ ہے کہ وہ طلاق اور غیر طلاق دونوں کا احتمال رکھتا ہو پس کنا یا اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی مراد پوشیدہ ہو بہ علامہ ابن نجیم المصری علیہ الرحمہ فرماتے ہیں کہ الفاظ کنائی سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے دیایہ نیت کا پایا جاتا ضروری ہے خواہ ندا کرۂ طلاق ہو یا نہ ہو ۔ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا اذہبی اور اس سے پہلے آپس میں دونوں میں تو تو میں میں ہور ہی تھی تو اگر چہ اسی صورت میں قضا ، تو وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہو گا خواہ شوہر طلاق کی نیت سے انکار کرے لیکن مائینہ طارق اسی وقت معتبر ہوگی جب شوہر یوں کہے کہ میں نے اذہبی سے طلاق ہی مراد لی ہے اور اگر اس نے طلاق کی نیت نہ کی تو دیانیت عدم وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہوگا۔
قضاء الفاظ کنائی سے طلاق اس وقت مراد ہوگی جب وہ نیت طلاق کی کرے یا کوئی قرینہ صراحت یا دالیا پایا جائے مثلا وہ صراحتہ کہے کہ اذ ہی سے میں نے طلاق ہی مراد لی ہے یا دونوں میں جھگڑا ہور ہا تھا اور آپس میں طارق کا ندا کرہ ہو رہا تھا اور ایسی حالت میں شوہر نے بیوی سے کہہ دیا اذ ہی تو قضاء وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہو گا بھلے شو ہر طارق کی نسیت کا انکار کرے کیونکہ قضاء تو ظاہر پر نافذ ہوتی ہے اور یہ دلالت حال ہی طلاق کے معنی کو ظاہر کر رہی ہے اور اگر شوہر نے اس سے طلاق کی نیت کی ہے تو دیا یہ بھی وقوع طلاق کا حکم ہو گا اس لئے کہ جب دریا ئیہ وقوع طلاق کا نظم ہے تو قضاء بدرجہ اول طلاق کا حکم ہوگا۔
دیا یہ وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے مذاکر کو طلاق ہو یا نہ ہو دونوں برابر ہیں۔ المذاكرة انما تقوم مقام النية الخ. اس عبارت سے صاحب کتاب یہ بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ الفاظ کنائی سے قضاء وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت ہو اور اگر نیت نہ ہو تو قرینہ یعنی مذاکرۂ طلاق وغیرہ ہو اس لئے کہ مذاکرۂ طلاق الفاظ کنائی سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے مثل نیت کے ہے۔ البتہ لفظ حرام اگر چہ الفاظ کنائی میں سے ہے لیکن یہ لفظ طلاق کے معنی میں صریح ہے اس لئے اس سے فورا طلاق کے سلسلہ میں نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ اور یہ دراصل استثناء ہے مصنف کی عبارت لا يقع بها الاسبانية سے لیکن اگر اس سے آپ طلاق بائن مراد لیں گے تو بینونت کے لئے نیت ضروری ہوگی اس لئے کہ مینونت کے اعتبار سے یہ الفاظ کنائی میں سے ہے اور الفاظ کنائی سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت ضروری ہے اور طلاق کے معنی میں مورتا ہے اس لئے لفظ حرام سے وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت پیش نہیں آئے گی ۔
اذا كان الزوج من قوم يريدون بالحرام الطلاق. اس عبارت سے ایک ضابطہ عرف کا بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ کوئی لفظ کنائی اگر کسی مقام پر یا کسی قبیلہ میں لفظ طلاق کے معنی میں صریح ہو تو وہاں عرف کا اعتبار ہوگا اور اس نقط کنائی سے اس مقام وقوع طلاق کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت پیش نہیں آئے گی اس لئے کہ اصول فقہ کا قائدا ؟
الثابت بالعرف كالثابت بالنص
وَأَمَّا تَفْوِيْضُ الطَّلَاقِ وَالْخَلْعُ وَالإيْلاءُ والظَّهَارُ فَمَا كَانَ مِنْهُ صَرِيْحاً لَا تُشْتَرَطَ لَهُ النَّبه
وَمَا كَانَ كِنَايَةً أُشْتَرِطَتْ لَهُ.
And it is necessary that the woman be described with the attribute of talaq by way of implication, and talaq is estimated to be intended. And in our madhhab, there is no generality, and we have estimated the implication for the correctness of speech. Since the speech is correct with one talaq, there remains no need for two or three. Therefore, intending three is intending a meaning that the word does not admit, so intending three is not valid. And the intention of two in the masdar is not valid. If someone says to his wife 'Anti al-talaq', then one talaq occurs, and if he intends three, three also occur because 'al-talaq' is a masdar and in the masdar there is unity, and unity is of two types: (1) essential (dhati) (2) generic (naw'i). So essential unity is realized without intention, but since the masdar also admits generic unity, for its affirmation intention is required. Thus three talaqs occur only when the speaker intends three; without intention three are not established. However, intention of two is not valid because two is a number and the masdar indicates unity, and number and unity are contradictory, and one cannot intend the opposite of a word. But if the wife is a slave, then in 'Anti al-talaq' two talaqs can be intended because for a slave two are generic unity. And as for his kinayah words, talaq does not occur by them except with intention in diyanah, whether or not there was prior discussion of talaq. And prior discussion (mudhakara) takes the place of intention in qada' except for the word 'haram', for it is kinayah and does not need it; it is turned to talaq when the husband is from a people who intend talaq by 'haram'. Translation: And however his kinayah, talaq occurs by them with intention in diyanah, whether or not there was mudhakara of talaq. And mudhakara stands in place of intention only in qada', except for the word 'haram' because it is kinayah and does not need it; so it is turned to talaq when the husband is from a people who intend talaq by 'haram'. Commentary: Kinayah linguistically is something by which something else is inferred or intended. Legally, it is what conceals its real or metaphorical meaning, for the abandoned literal meaning is kinayah like the dominant metaphorical usage. (Sharh al-Hamawi with al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, p. 79). By kinayah is meant that it admits both talaq and non-talaq. So kinayah is that whose meaning is hidden. Allamah Ibn Nujaym al-Misri (may Allah have mercy on him) says that for occurrence of talaq by kinayah words, intention is necessary in diyanah, whether there was mudhakara of talaq or not. If someone says to his wife 'Udhhabi' and before that there was a dispute between them, then in qada' the ruling of talaq will be given even if the husband denies intention. But in diyanah, his statement is accepted only if he says 'I intended talaq by udhhabi'; if he did not intend talaq, then in diyanah the ruling is non-occurrence of talaq. In qada', talaq is intended by kinayah words when he makes intention of talaq or there is a clear qarina (indication) — for example, he explicitly says 'I intended talaq by udhhabi' or there was a quarrel and discussion of talaq between them, and in such a state the husband said 'udhhabi' to his wife, then in qada' the ruling of talaq occurs even if the husband denies intention because qada' is based on apparent circumstances, and this contextual indication shows the meaning of talaq. And if the husband intended talaq, then in diyanah also the ruling of talaq occurs because when diyanah is the foundation of occurrence of talaq, then a fortiori qada' will rule talaq. In diyanah, for occurrence of talaq, intention is necessary, whether mudhakara of talaq existed or not, both are equal. 'Al-mudhakara innama taqumu maqam al-niyyah' etc. With this phrase, the author explains that for occurrence of talaq by kinayah in qada', intention is required, and if there is no intention, then qarina like mudhakara of talaq suffices because mudhakara of talaq stands in place of intention for kinayah. However, the word 'haram' although from kinayah, is clear in the meaning of talaq, so for talaq it does not require intention. This is actually an exception to the author's statement 'la yaqa'u biha illa bil-niyyah'. But if one intends talaq ba'in by it, then for ba'in intention is necessary because in terms of ba'in it is from kinayah, and for kinayah words intention is required for occurrence of talaq. Since it is commonly used in the meaning of talaq, for occurrence of talaq by the word 'haram' no intention is needed. 'Idha kana al-zawju min qawmin yuriduna bil-haram al-talaq': With this phrase, a rule of custom is being explained: if a kinayah word in a place or in a tribe is clear in the meaning of talaq, then custom will be considered, and that kinayah will not require intention for occurrence of talaq in that place, because the principle of usul al-fiqh: Al-thabit bil-urf ka al-thabit bil-nass (What is established by custom is like what is established by textual evidence). And as for tafwid al-talaq, khul', ila', and zihar: whatever of them is explicit, intention is not required for it, and whatever is kinayah, intention is required for it.

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule discussing the conditions for talaq to occur with kinayah (implicit) words. The author begins by addressing a specific case: if a husband says 'Anti al-talaq' (You are divorce), which is a masdar (verbal noun). According to Hanafi fiqh, this expression results in one talaq automatically due to the essential unity of the masdar. If the husband intends three talaqs, they occur because the masdar can also indicate generic unity, but that requires intention. However, intending two talaqs is invalid because two is a number contradicting the unity implied by the masdar, unless the wife is a slave, where two is considered generic unity. The passage then turns to the general rule for kinayah: such words require niyyah (intention) for talaq to take effect in diyanah (in the sight of Allah), regardless of whether the couple had been discussing talaq (mudhakara). However, in qada' (court), mudhakara takes the place of niyyah. The exception is the word 'haram': if the husband's community customarily uses 'haram' to mean talaq, then no intention is needed; it is treated as sarih (explicit). The author then defines kinayah linguistically and legally, citing Ibn Nujaym, and elaborates with examples like saying 'Udhhabi' (Go away). If there is a prior dispute or context indicating talaq, the court rules talaq occurred, even if the husband denies intention. But in diyanah, his word is taken unless he concedes intention. Finally, the passage introduces the maxim 'Al-thabit bil-urf ka al-thabit bil-nass' (What is established by custom is like what is established by textual evidence), applying it to the word 'haram'. The passage concludes by listing other types of divorce (tafwid, khul', ila', zihar) with the same rule: explicit forms need no intention, kinayah forms need intention. Throughout, the focus is on the practical fatwa rules for a mufti or judge determining the occurrence of talaq based on apparent circumstances and custom.

Key Terms
مصدر (masdar): A verbal noun that indicates the action itself without reference to time or number, but in Arabic it inherently indicates a single occurrence (essential unity).
توحد ذاتی (wahdah dhatiyyah): Essential unity: the inherent indication of one occurrence in a masdar.
توحد نوعی (wahdah naw'iyyah): Generic unity: the indication of the entire type or category; requires intention to be realized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the intention of two talaqs invalid when saying 'Anti al-talaq' to a free wife?
A) Because the masdar can only indicate one or three, not two.
B) Because two is a number that contradicts the essential unity indicated by the masdar.
C) Because the wife must consent to two talaqs.
D) Because two talaqs are not recognized in the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
When using a masdar like 'al-talaq', one talaq is automatic; only three can be intended (for a free wife); two is invalid due to linguistic contradiction.

Act of the Person on Whom the Oath is Taken: Intention Irrelevant
Ruling that the act of the person on whom the oath is taken is not conditioned by intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the act of the person on whom an oath is taken (المحلوف عليه) triggers the oath's consequence based solely on external occurrence, regardless of intention, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In oaths, the external act of the person on whom the oath is taken triggers the consequence—intention is irrelevant.

This lesson clarifies a key principle in Hanafi oath jurisprudence: the ruling on the act of the person on whom an oath is taken (المحلوف عليه) does not depend on their intention. The passage provides two vivid examples involving divorce and a promise not to enter a shop, demonstrating that the oath is fulfilled or broken by the mere external occurrence of the condition, whether the person acted intentionally, forgetfully, mistakenly, or under compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Define key terms
Identify the terms 'المحلوف عليه' (the person/thing on whom the oath is taken) and 'الحانث' (the breaker of the oath). Understand that the first is the subject whose action triggers the oath, and the second is the person who swore the oath.
In the examples, the المحلوف عليه is the entering person or the shop; the الحانث is the one who swore.
2
State the rule
Extract the core legal principle: the act of the المحلوف عليه is not conditioned on intention (نية). The hukm (divorce or breaking the oath) occurs purely by the external occurrence of the act.
This is a qaw'ida (legal maxim) applicable in oaths of divorce, manumission, and vows.
3
Apply the rule to the first example
Consider the case: 'If Zaid enters the house, then you are divorced.' Apply the rule: divorce occurs whenever Zaid enters, regardless of his intent, forgetfulness, mistake, or coercion. Identify the key word 'خوا' (whether) in the text.
The text explicitly lists intentional, forgetful, mistaken, and forced entry—all cause divorce.
4
Apply the rule to the second example
Consider the case: 'By Allah, I will not go to so-and-so's shop.' If the swearer goes to that shop, he becomes حانث even if he went forgetfully, mistakenly, or was forced. The act itself is decisive.
The shop is the محلوف عليه (the thing on which the oath is taken). Its existence is irrelevant; only the act of going matters.
5
Identify the fatwa-bihi position
Confirm that this ruling is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school. It is established in zahir al-riwayah and consistently applied in fatwa.
There is no significant intra-Hanafi dispute on this point.

Source Text

وكذا إذا فعل المحلوف عليه : المحلوف عليه جس کی قسم کھائی گئی ہے اس کا فعل بھی نیت پر موقوف نہیں ہے۔ مثلاً کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ فلاں شخص اگر گھر میں آ گیا تو تجھے طلاق، تو اب جب بھی وہ گھر میں آئے گا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی، چاہے آتے وقت اس فلاں کے گھر میں آنے کی نیت ہو یا نہ ہو، اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا۔ خواہ وہ عمداً ہو یا بھول کر یا غلطی سے ہو یا بجبر و اکراہ گھر میں اس فلاں کو لایا گیا ہو۔ دوسرا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مثلاً مخلوف علیہ یعنی جس پر قسم کھائی گئی ہے وہ کسی کی دوکان ہے، قسم کھانے والے نے قسم کھائی کہ اللہ کی قسم میں فلاں کی دوکان پر نہیں جاؤں گا، اب وہ دوکان پر چلا گیا، خواہ عمداً دوکان پر گیا یا بھول کر یا غلطی سے یا بجبر و اکراہ، ہر صورت میں حانث ہو جائے گا۔
And likewise, if the person on whom the oath is taken acts: the act of the one on whom the oath is taken, i.e., the one regarding whom the oath was sworn, his act is also not dependent on intention. For example, someone swore that if such a person enters the house, then you are divorced; so whenever he enters the house, divorce will occur, whether at the time of entry that person intended to enter or not—it will not be considered. Whether that person entered intentionally, forgetfully, mistakenly, or was brought into the house by force or coercion. The second meaning is that, for example, the one on whom the oath is taken is someone's shop; the one swearing took an oath saying, 'By Allah, I will not go to so-and-so's shop.' Now he went to the shop, whether intentional, forgetful, mistaken, or forced—in all cases he will become a breaker of the oath.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling that the act of the person on whom an oath is taken (المحلوف عليه) is not dependent on intention. The legal effect (hukm) is determined by the external act itself, regardless of the actor's intent. The author gives two examples. First: If someone swears, 'If so-and-so enters the house, then you are divorced,' then whenever that person enters, divorce occurs, whether he intended to enter or not, and whether he entered voluntarily, forgetfully, mistakenly, or was forced. Second: If someone swears, 'By Allah, I will not go to so-and-so's shop,' and then goes to that shop, he becomes a breaker of the oath (hanith) regardless of his intention. This rule applies because the oath is based on the external occurrence of the condition, not on the inner state of the person whose act triggers it. This is a well-established principle in Hanafi fiqh for oaths, particularly those involving divorce or manumission.

Key Terms
المحلوف عليه (al-mahluuf alayhi): The person or thing regarding whom an oath is taken; the one whose action triggers the oath's consequence.
الحانث (al-haanith): The one who breaks his oath.
فعل المحلوف عليه (fi'l al-mahluuf alayhi): The act of the person/thing on whom the oath is taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs because Zaid did not intend to enter.
B) Divorce occurs because the act of entering happened.
C) It depends on Zaid's intention.
D) The oath becomes invalid due to coercion.

Key Takeaway
The act of the person on whom an oath is taken is judged by external occurrence alone; intention is irrelevant.
